        <a href="https://www.muscleforlife.com/services/custom-meal-plan">
         Custom Meal Plans
        </a>
       </li>
       <li class="about menu-item menu-item-type-post_type menu-item-object-page menu-item-159" id="menu-item-159">

         About
        </a>
       </li>

     The Ultimate Chest Workout: Chest Exercises for Awesome Pecs
    </h1>
    <span class="singlePostAuthor">

     <span itemprop="author" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
      <span itemprop="name">
       <a href="https://www.muscleforlife.com/about-me/" rel="author external" target="_blank" title="Visit Michael Matthewss website">
        Michael Matthews
       </a>
      </span>
     </span>
    </span>

     <div class="postWrap image-post ">
      <div class="postImage">
       <span class="pibfi_pinterest">
        <img alt="The Ultimate Chest Workout: Chest Exercises for Awesome Pecs" class="pinthis" itemprop="image" src="http://www.muscleforlife.com/wp-content/themes/mfl/tim/timthumb.php?src=http://www.muscleforlife.com/wp-content/uploads/2013/12/mike-matthews-chest-shot.jpg&amp;w=640&amp;q=85"/>
        <span class="xc_pin" onclick="pin_this(event, 'http://pinterest.com/pin/create/button/?url=http://www.muscleforlife.com/chest-workout-best-chest-exercises/&amp;media=http://www.muscleforlife.com/wp-content/uploads/2013/12/mike-matthews-chest-shot.jpg&amp;description=The+best+types+of+chest+workouts+and+chest+exercises+aren%27t+what+most+people+believe.+In+this+article%2C+you%27re+going+to+learn+what+it+really+takes+to+build+full%2C+strong+pecs.')" style="left: 492px; display: block;">
        </span>

      <div class="singlePostContent article">
       <h1 style="text-align: center;">
        The best types of chest workouts and chest exercises arent what most people believe. In this article, youre going to learn what it really takes to build full, strong pecs.
       </h1>
       <p>
       </p>
       <p>
        Help, my chest is too small!
       </p>
       <p>
        I receive those words, or something similar, at least 10 times per week. Its by far the most common complaint among the guys that email and message me asking for help.
       </p>
       <p>
        And I understand. Building a big, strong chest can be quite tough if youre focusing on the wrong chest exercises and rep ranges (and if your nutrition is off, of course).
       </p>
       <p>
        In this article, Im going to share with you the chest exercises that have not only helped me build a full, strong chest, but have helped many of my readers and followers do the same.
       </p>
       <h3 style="text-align: center;">
        <strong>
         You Dont Just Want to Build a Big ChestYou Want a Full, Proportionate Chest
        </strong>
       </h3>
       <p>
        The first thing I want to address is the goal.Simply having a big chest shouldnt be the goal, because just adding size willy-nilly wont necessarily give you the look you want.
       </p>
       <p>
        The most common mistake we want to avoid is building a big lower chest and small upper chest. Heres an example of this:
       </p>
       <p style="text-align: center;">
        <img alt="no-upper-chest" class="size-full wp-image-10596 aligncenter" height="375" src="https://www.muscleforlife.com/wp-content/uploads/2013/12/no-upper-chest.jpg" width="500"/>
       </p>
       <p>
        Now, he doesnt have a bad physique, and has clearly been working hard for at least a couple of years. But take a closer look at his chest. All his mass is on the lower, outer portions of he pecs, with little-to-none in the upper, inner portions.
       </p>
       <p>
        Compare that now to a picture of Greg Plitts chest:
       </p>
       <p style="text-align: center;">
        <span class="pibfi_pinterest ">
         <img alt="greg-plitt-chest" class="size-full wp-image-10597 aligncenter pinthis" height="447" src="https://www.muscleforlife.com/wp-content/uploads/2013/12/greg-plitt-chest.jpg" width="465"/>
         <span class="xc_pin" onclick="pin_this(event, 'http://pinterest.com/pin/create/button/?url=http://www.muscleforlife.com/chest-workout-best-chest-exercises/&amp;media=https://www.muscleforlife.com/wp-content/uploads/2013/12/greg-plitt-chest.jpg&amp;description=http://www.muscleforlife.com/chest-workout-best-chest-exercises/')">
         </span>
        </span>
       </p>
       <p>
        While the overall physiques arent remotely comparable, again look to the chest Its actually not THAT much larger than the first, but its much better developed. But whats the major difference here?
        <em>
         The upper and inner portions of the chest.
        </em>
       </p>
       <p>
        But wait a minute, you might be thinking. Isnt the whole upper and lower chest thing a myth?
       </p>
       <p>
        Well, lets take a moment to address that.
       </p>
       <h3 style="text-align: center;">
        <strong>
         The Truth About the Upper Chest and Lower Chest
        </strong>
       </h3>
       <p>
        The upper chest debate has been going on for a long time.
       </p>
       <p>
        Do you need to do chest exercises specifically for the upper chest? Or do all chest exercises stimulate all available muscle fibers? And even more to the point, is there even such a thing as the upper chest?
       </p>
       <p>
        Well, Ill keep this short and sweet.
       </p>
       <p>
        First, yes, there is a muscle that forms what we call the upper chest. Its known as the
        <em>
         clavicular pectoralis.
        </em>
        Heres what it looks like:
       </p>
       <div class="wp-caption aligncenter" id="attachment_10603" style="width: 280px">
        <span class="pibfi_pinterest ">
         <img alt="upper-chest" class="size-full wp-image-10603 pinthis" height="536" src="https://www.muscleforlife.com/wp-content/uploads/2013/12/upper-chest.gif" width="270"/>
         <span class="xc_pin" onclick="pin_this(event, 'http://pinterest.com/pin/create/button/?url=http://www.muscleforlife.com/chest-workout-best-chest-exercises/&amp;media=https://www.muscleforlife.com/wp-content/uploads/2013/12/upper-chest.gif&amp;description=http://www.muscleforlife.com/chest-workout-best-chest-exercises/')">
         </span>
        </span>
        <p class="wp-caption-text">
         clavicular pectoralis for upper chest
        </p>
       </div>
       <p>
        <strong>
         Despite what people might tell you, this muscle is
         <em>
          not
         </em>
         apart of the big chest muscle, the
         <em>
          pectoralis major.
         </em>
        </strong>
        While part of the pectoralis major shares nerves with the clavicular pectoralis, the angle of the muscle fibers varies greatly. Thus, certain movements can emphasize the pectoralis major, whereas others can emphasize the clavicular pectoralis.
       </p>
       <p>
        Notice that I say
        <em>
         emphasize
        </em>
        , not
        <em>
         isolate
        </em>
        . Thats because all movements that emphasize one of the two do, to some degree, involve the other.
        <strong>
         But the bottom line is proper chest development requires a
         <em>
          lot
         </em>
         of emphasis on the clavicular pectoralis for two simple reasons:
        </strong>
       </p>
       <ol>
        <li>
         <span style="line-height: 13px;">
          Its a small, stubborn muscle that takes its sweet time to grow.
         </span>
        </li>
        <li>
         The movements that are best for developing it also happen to be great for growing the pectoralis major as well.
        </li>
       </ol>
       <p>
        Curious how this plays out in the real world? Well, lets look to my own body as an example. First, check out the following picture of me, taken about 2 years ago:
       </p>
       <p style="text-align: center;">
        <span class="pibfi_pinterest ">
         <img alt="chest workout" class=" aligncenter pinthis" height="584" src="https://www.muscleforlife.com/wp-content/uploads/2013/12/IMG_20130320_181228.jpg" width="584"/>
         <span class="xc_pin" onclick="pin_this(event, 'http://pinterest.com/pin/create/button/?url=http://www.muscleforlife.com/chest-workout-best-chest-exercises/&amp;media=https://www.muscleforlife.com/wp-content/uploads/2013/12/IMG_20130320_181228.jpg&amp;description=http://www.muscleforlife.com/chest-workout-best-chest-exercises/')">
         </span>
        </span>
       </p>
       <p>
        I looked decent, but look at the upper portion of my left pec (the right looks bigger than it is because of how Im holding the phone). As you can see, I had a very bottom-heavy chest with not much to show for upstairs.
       </p>
       <p>
        I started addressing this by following the chest workouts Im going to share you with later in this article, and this was the result:
       </p>
       <p style="text-align: center;">
        <span class="pibfi_pinterest ">
         <img alt="best chest exercises" class="size-full wp-image-10610 aligncenter pinthis" height="792" src="https://www.muscleforlife.com/wp-content/uploads/2013/12/mike-matthews-upper-chest.jpg" width="499"/>
         <span class="xc_pin" onclick="pin_this(event, 'http://pinterest.com/pin/create/button/?url=http://www.muscleforlife.com/chest-workout-best-chest-exercises/&amp;media=https://www.muscleforlife.com/wp-content/uploads/2013/12/mike-matthews-upper-chest.jpg&amp;description=http://www.muscleforlife.com/chest-workout-best-chest-exercises/')">
         </span>
        </span>
       </p>
       <p>
        See how much of a difference a full upper chest makes? And yes, that transformation was accomplished by doing
        <em>
         exactly
        </em>
        what Im going to share with you here, and nothing else.
       </p>
       <p>
        So, lets get right to it then
       </p>
       <h3 style="text-align: center;">
        <strong>
         The Ultimate Chest Workout:
        </strong>
        <br/>
        <strong>
         The Chest Exercises That Build Outstanding Pecs
        </strong>
       </h3>
       <p style="text-align: left;">
        I go over the science of this full on my
        <a href="https://www.muscleforlife.com/guide-to-muscle-hypertrophy-muscle-growth/">
         article on muscle hypertrophy (muscle growth)
        </a>
        , but heres the first thing you need to know:
       </p>
       <p style="text-align: left;">
        <strong>
         If you want to build a big, strong chest,the majority of your reps should be performed with 80  85% of your one-rep max (1RM).
        </strong>
        This is the 4  6 or 5  7 rep range.
       </p>
       <p>
        Pump away in the 10+ rep range all you want, and youll never have a great chest. I guarantee it. Thats why youre going to see a lot of heavy weightlifting in my routine.
       </p>
       <p style="text-align: center;">
        <span style="text-decoration: underline;">
         <strong>
          The Best Chest Exercises
         </strong>
        </span>
       </p>
       <p style="text-align: left;">
        The best chest exercises are few, and accomplish a very simple task: they maximally recruit muscle fibers, and allow for heavy, progressive overload without dramatically increasing the risk of injury. These exercises are
       </p>
       <ul>
        <li>
         Barbell Bench Press (Incline and Flat, free weight not Smith Machine)
        </li>
        <li>
         Dumbbell Bench Press (Incline and Flat)
        </li>
        <li>
         Dips (
         <a href="http://www.bodybuilding.com/exercises/detail/view/name/dips-chest-version" target="_blank">
          Chest Version
         </a>
         , weighted if possible)
        </li>
       </ul>
       <p style="text-align: left;">
        <strong>
         These are the exercises you
         <em>
          must
         </em>
         master if you want to build an impressive chest. Period.
        </strong>
        Forget cable work, dumbbell flys, push-up variations, machines, and every other type of chest exercise out there. They are not nearly as effective as the above three core, foundation-building lifts.
       </p>
       <p style="text-align: left;">
        Why no Smith Machine, you ask? Simply because
        <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/20093960" target="_blank">
         research
        </a>
        has proven it inferior to free weight exercises in terms of muscle recruitment.
       </p>
       <p style="text-align: left;">
        And why no decline pressing? Because the decline press not only reduces the range of motion of the exercise, thus
        <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/22027847" target="_blank">
         reducing the amount of work your muscles have to do
        </a>
        , it places
        <i>
         maximum
        </i>
        emphasis on the pectoralis major and minimum emphasis on the clavicular pectoralis, which simply isnt ideal (if you want to build a really droopy, bottom-heavy chest, do a ton of flat and decline pressing and no incline pressing).
       </p>
       <p style="text-align: left;">
        Now, many people are surprised to hear this advice of mine, and are even more surprised when they see my pictures and hear that was accomplished by doing
        <em>
         nothing
        </em>
        but the exercises listed above.
        <strong>
         Thats rightnot a single fly, cable crossover, or machine rep was done.
        </strong>
       </p>
       <p style="text-align: left;">
        Is building an awesome chest really that simple? Yep, it is.
       </p>
       <p style="text-align: left;">
        (That said, I do think dumbbell flys and cable work has a place in the routine of an advanced weightlifter that has already built a big, strong chest, but well save that for another article. In order to
        <em>
         get
        </em>
        to that point, it only requires the above.)
       </p>
       <p style="text-align: left;">
        <strong>
         The key isnt just
         <em>
          doing
         </em>
         the above exercises, however. Its
         <em>
          progressing
         </em>
         on them.
        </strong>
        That is, increasing the amount of weight you can push over time. If you dont get stronger, you wont get bigger.
       </p>
       <p style="text-align: left;">
        Now, a few tips in performing these exercises:
       </p>
       <p style="text-align: center;">
        <span style="text-decoration: underline;">
         Barbell Bench Press
        </span>
       </p>
       <p style="text-align: left;">
        Many people worry that the Barbell Bench Press puts your shoulders at a high risk of injury. This is true
        <em>
         only
        </em>
        if your form is improper.
       </p>
       <p style="text-align: left;">
        Here are the two major points of form that protect your shoulders when youre performing the Barbell Bench Press:
       </p>
       <p style="padding-left: 30px;">
        <strong style="line-height: 13px;">
         1. Keep your elbows at a 20  30 degree angle relative to your torso.
        </strong>
        <span style="line-height: 13px;">
         The most common mistake people make is they flare their elbows out, sometimes approaching 90 degrees relative to their torsos. This dramatically increases the stress on your shoulders.
        </span>
       </p>
       <p style="padding-left: 30px;">
        In case youre not sure what the angles look like, heres a picture from my book
        <em>
         <a href="https://www.muscleforlife.com/books/bigger-leaner-stronger/">
          Bigger Leaner Stronger
         </a>
         :
        </em>
       </p>
       <p>
        <span class="pibfi_pinterest ">
         <img alt="bench-press-form" class="aligncenter size-full wp-image-10614 pinthis" height="386" src="https://www.muscleforlife.com/wp-content/uploads/2013/12/bench-press-form.png" width="500"/>
         <span class="xc_pin" onclick="pin_this(event, 'http://pinterest.com/pin/create/button/?url=http://www.muscleforlife.com/chest-workout-best-chest-exercises/&amp;media=https://www.muscleforlife.com/wp-content/uploads/2013/12/bench-press-form.png&amp;description=http://www.muscleforlife.com/chest-workout-best-chest-exercises/')">
         </span>
        </span>
       </p>
       <p style="padding-left: 30px;">
        The position where the hands are closest to the torso puts the arms at about 20 degrees relative to the torso. The next position out is about 45 degrees. And the furthermost position is 90 degrees.
       </p>
       <p style="padding-left: 30px;">
        <strong>
         2. Keep your shoulder blades pinched and your back slightly arched.
        </strong>
        You dont want to flatten your chest out at the bottom of the lift, rolling your shoulders. Instead, your shoulder blades should always remain tightly pinched, which pushes your chest up, and you should always have enough arch in your lower back to fit a fist in the pocket between it and the bench.
       </p>
       <p>
        The final point of form that you should know is
        <strong>
         the bar must touch your chest every rep.
        </strong>
        Stopping short reduces the range of motion, which as you know, means less gains.
       </p>
       <p style="text-align: center;">
        <span style="text-decoration: underline;">
         Dumbbell Bench Press
        </span>
       </p>
       <p>
        One of the big advantages of the Dumbbell Bench Press is that it allows you to increase the range of motion beyond the barbell press. Heres how I like to perform the Dumbbell Bench Press (this is incline, of course, but you get the idea):
       </p>
       <p>
        <iframe frameborder="0" height="710" scrolling="no" src="//instagram.com/p/gA754PRUbc/embed/" width="612">
        </iframe>
       </p>
       <p>
        Technically my butt shouldnt be movingI was trying to move up in weight here and got a little overzealousbut what I wanted to show you was how I rotate my hands at the bottom of the rep and bring the dumbbells low. This increases the range of motion without increasing the risk of injury, and Ive found this very helpful in progressing with the weight and developing my chest.
       </p>
       <h3 style="text-align: center;">
        <strong>
         The Ultimate Chest Workout
        </strong>
       </h3>
       <p style="text-align: left;">
        A good chest workout trains the entire chest, with emphasis on the upper chest, and focuses on heavy weights. Just like any other muscle group, the chest can benefit from higher rep work, but you have to emphasize the heavy weightlifting if you want them to grow.
       </p>
       <p style="text-align: left;">
        <strong>
         While I go over everything you need to program your own leg workouts in
         <em>
          <a href="https://www.muscleforlife.com/books/bigger-leaner-stronger/">
           Bigger Leaner Stronger
          </a>
         </em>
         and
         <em>
          <a href="https://www.muscleforlife.com/books/thinner-leaner-stronger/">
           Thinner Leaner Stronger
          </a>
         </em>
         (and provide you with an entire years worth of workouts that can, when combined with proper nutrition, help you put on 20  25 pounds of muscle in your first year of weightlifting), I want to leave you with a chest workout that will prove the effectiveness of what Ive discussed in this article.
        </strong>
       </p>
       <p style="text-align: left;">
        What I want you to do over the next 8 weeks is perform the following chest workout once every 5  7 days:
       </p>
       <blockquote>
        <p style="text-align: center;">
         <strong>
          <em>
           Incline Barbell Bench Press: Warm up and 3 sets of 4  6 reps
          </em>
         </strong>
        </p>
        <p style="text-align: center;">
         <strong>
          <em>
           Incline Dumbbell Bench Press: 3setsof 4  6 reps
          </em>
         </strong>
        </p>
        <p style="text-align: center;">
         <strong>
          <em>
           Flat Barbell Bench Press: 3 sets of 4  6 reps
          </em>
         </strong>
        </p>
       </blockquote>
       <p style="text-align: left;">
        <span style="text-align: left;">
         Thats itjust 9 heavy sets for your entire workout. If youre an advanced lifter, or you feel you have more in you at the end of the workout, you can add 3 more sets (one more exercise, in this case, Dips), but dont do more than that or you will likely wind up
         <a href="https://www.muscleforlife.com/8-signs-of-overtraining/">
          overtrained
         </a>
         at some point.
        </span>
       </p>
       <p style="text-align: left;">
        <b>
         Rest 2  3 minutes in between each set.
        </b>
        This will give your muscles enough time to fully recoup their strength so you can give maximum effort each set.
       </p>
       <p style="text-align: left;">
        <span style="text-align: left;">
         <strong>
          Once you hit the top of your rep range for one set, you move up in weight.
         </strong>
         For instance, if you get on the incline bench and push out 6 reps on your first set, you add 5 pounds to each side of the bar for your next set and work with that weight until you can press it for 6 reps, and so forth.
        </span>
       </p>
       <p style="text-align: left;">
       </p>
       <div class="yellow-cta">
        <div class="yellow-cta-container">
         <p>
          <strong>
           Want more workouts like this plus a flexible diet plan?
          </strong>
          Download my free no-BS crash course now and learn exactly how to build the body of your dreams.
         </p>
         <div class="aligncenter">
          <a class="popup-link btn-access" href="#getAccess">

          </a>
         </div>
        </div>
       </div>
       <p style="text-align: left;">
        <strong>
         I guarantee you that if you combine that chest workout with a
         <a href="https://www.muscleforlife.com/the-best-way-to-gain-muscle-not-fat/">

         </a>

         <em>
          very
         </em>
         happy with how your chest responds.
        </strong>
        This type of training is the core of my
        <a href="https://www.muscleforlife.com/books/bigger-leaner-stronger/">
         <em>
          Bigger Leaner Stronger
         </em>
         program
        </a>

       </p>

             <p>
              Mike,
             </p>
             <p>
              Just great advice on the 20-30 degree arm angle to take stress off of the shoulders.
             </p>
             <p>
              Tucking the elbows will result in the bar contacting lower on the chest.
             </p>
             <p>
              Since you cant rotate the weights on an Incline Barbell Press (like you did with the incline dumbbell press in the video), I assume you tuck your elbows on the Incline Barbell Press also?
              <br/>
              I ask because I have often heard people say that on IBP the bar needs to contact high on the chest to work the upper chest.  It seems like that old advice kills the shoulders though!
             </p>
            </div>

               <ul class="children">
                <li class="comment odd alt depth-4" id="dsq-comment-7500">
                 <div class="dsq-comment-header" id="dsq-comment-header-7500">
                  <cite id="dsq-cite-7500">
                   <span id="dsq-author-user-7500">
                    Michael Matthews
                   </span>
                  </cite>
                 </div>
                 <div class="dsq-comment-body" id="dsq-comment-body-7500">
                  <div class="dsq-comment-message" id="dsq-comment-message-7500">
                   <p>
                    Slow chest growth is probably the issue Im most often emailed about, haha. This is definitely the most common genetic weak point, and really just takes time and hard work to get through. In my opinion, it takes 1-2 years to build what we would consider a solid chest, and 3-4 to build an awesome chest.
                   </p>
                   <p>
                    Dont be discouraged by that though. As long as your diet is right and youre getting stronger each month, your chest WILL grow. It just grows slower than most people prefer.
                   </p>
                   <p>
                    Heres one little trick you can do:
                   </p>
                   <p>
                    On the 3rd day after chest day, start your workout with a chest warm-up and 3 heavy sets of incline barbell press. Then move on to your normal workout. This wont be enough to interfere with your next chest day, and can give you a little boost.
                   </p>
                   <p>
                    Soreness isnt necessarily an indicator of a good workout. Genetics, nutrition, and conditioning all play a role.
                   </p>
                   <p>
                    Check out my article on this:
                   </p>
                   <p>
                    <a href="https://www.muscleforlife.com" rel="nofollow">
                     https://www.muscleforlife.com
                    </a>
                    /reduce-muscle-soreness/
                   </p>
                   <p>
                    As long as youre getting in your 9-12 heavy sets per workout with good form, youre doing it right. And you should see results to prove ityour strength should go up and you should gain muscle. That said, if youve been training for quite some time, I recommend bumping the workouts up by 3 more sets. Just add one more exercise, and do 3 sets.
                   </p>
                  </div>
                 </div>
                 <ul class="children">

             <p>
              Im glad I found this article but I have a question about the progression. You mentioned that as long as we can do one set of 6 out of the three then we should add another 5 pounds. I thought in the book you mentioned that we should only move up in weight if we can do 3 consecutive sets of 6 rep?
             </p>

           <ul class="children">
            <li class="comment even depth-2" id="dsq-comment-6888">
             <div class="dsq-comment-header" id="dsq-comment-header-6888">
              <cite id="dsq-cite-6888">
               <span id="dsq-author-user-6888">
                Michael Matthews
               </span>
              </cite>
             </div>
             <div class="dsq-comment-body" id="dsq-comment-body-6888">
              <div class="dsq-comment-message" id="dsq-comment-message-6888">
               <p>
                Thanks Mario!
               </p>
               <p>
                Sorry for the confusion. I didnt make this clear enough in the book. You want to move up in weight after hitting your FIRST set of 6 reps. Then you add 10 pounds (5 pounds to each size of the bar; each dumbbell).
               </p>
               <p>
                The reality is training to 3 sets of 6 reps isnt BADit will still workbut youll make faster strength gains doing the above.
               </p>
               <p>
                Im going to make this clearer in the second edition of BLS, which I will be starting on soon.
               </p>
              </div>
             </div>

                   </p>

                    <br/>
                    5-4-4
                    <br/>
                    5-5-4
                    <br/>
                    5-5-5
                    <br/>
                    6-move up-4-4
                   </p>
                   <p>
                    Etc.
                   </p>
                   <p>
                    Of course its not always nice and linear but it is more often than it isnt.
                   </p>

             <p>
              Hi Michael  another great article.  Quick question  Ive been gradually more and more of your recommendations  low reps at 80% etc, diet.the one thing that Im still doing differently to you is how I break down my exercises in the schedule.  Im trying to plan a new routine based on what you advise in your BLS book  in the past Ive done four days with two varying workouts.  Mon &amp; Thur is chest &amp; tris, rest Wed, Tues &amp; Fri are back / shoulders and biceps.  Abs were on each session.
              <br/>
              Your advice on reps in the 4-6 range has been great  but Im still struggling to get the results I want  particularly in the chest.  So Im planning to give your five day schedule a go.  You only recommend doing chest once per week  did I understand that right?  If its a problem muscle group for me, should I be doing it more than once?  Same with abs  Ive always done them on every session at the beginning and end  just twice then?

           <ul class="children">
            <li class="comment even depth-2" id="dsq-comment-6979">
             <div class="dsq-comment-header" id="dsq-comment-header-6979">
              <cite id="dsq-cite-6979">
               <span id="dsq-author-user-6979">
                Michael Matthews
               </span>
              </cite>
             </div>
             <div class="dsq-comment-body" id="dsq-comment-body-6979">
              <div class="dsq-comment-message" id="dsq-comment-message-6979">
               <p>
                Thanks Jon!
               </p>
               <p>
                If youre giving your major muscle groups 4-5 days of rest before training them again, you can do that. But you would have to switch to a push-pull-legs routine so you dont skip shoulders or legs.
               </p>
               <p>
                That said, you can also stick to my BLS routine and just add in some weak point training on Thurs or Fri, where you start with a warm up and 6 heavy sets of chest, and then move on to your regular workout.
               </p>
              </div>

           <div class="dsq-comment-header" id="dsq-comment-header-6964">
            <cite id="dsq-cite-6964">
             <span id="dsq-author-user-6964">
              Carmine Pari
             </span>
            </cite>
           </div>
           <div class="dsq-comment-body" id="dsq-comment-body-6964">
            <div class="dsq-comment-message" id="dsq-comment-message-6964">
             <p>
              Thanks Mike. Over the past 5 months my chest has improved more than it has over the past 30 years. I am in great shape for my age, but I never had a great chest. Now I think my chest is looking pretty good. I added two things to my Mike Matthews workout that has really improved my chest: I decreased the angle of my incline benching from about 45 degrees to about 20 or 25 degree, and I added the decline dumbbell presses. I found the higher angle on the incline benching was working more shoulder. Decreasing the angle seems to work more of my upper chest. I did not have a nice cup and sides, but the decline dumbbell presses have improved them. I also do the dips as you recommend, of course.
             </p>
            </div>
           </div>
           <ul class="children">
            <li class="comment even depth-2" id="dsq-comment-6976">
             <div class="dsq-comment-header" id="dsq-comment-header-6976">

              <div class="dsq-comment-message" id="dsq-comment-message-6976">
               <p>
                Awesome Carmine, Im really glad to hear it.
               </p>
               <p>
                I also prefer a 30-degree angle on the incline because too high places too much emphasis on the shoulders.
               </p>
               <p>
                Interesting on the decline presses. Ive never been a fan because they have a reduce range of motion and the pec major is better stimulated by the flat and incline presses.
               </p>
               <p>
                Yeah I prefer dips over decline because it helps build the sides and bottom, but also works the rest of the pecs.
               </p>
               <p>
                Keep up the good work!
               </p>

                You want to do 9-12 heavy sets per chest workout if youre going to follow my advice. Stick to the 4-6 rep range.
               </p>
              </div>
             </div>

           <ul class="children">
            <li class="comment even depth-2" id="dsq-comment-9127">
             <div class="dsq-comment-header" id="dsq-comment-header-9127">
              <cite id="dsq-cite-9127">
               <span id="dsq-author-user-9127">
                Michael Matthews
               </span>
              </cite>
             </div>
             <div class="dsq-comment-body" id="dsq-comment-body-9127">
              <div class="dsq-comment-message" id="dsq-comment-message-9127">
               <p>
                Thanks!
               </p>
               <p>
                Like ideal rep ranges, optimal training frequency is a hotly debated subject. The bottom line is it boils down to workout intensity and volume. The lighter the weights and fewer the sets, the more often you can train the muscle group.
               </p>
               <p>
                In the case of BLS, you hit your muscles hard, with about 50-60 reps per workout, with all reps recruiting maximum muscle fibers (due to the load). The reality is unless you have superhuman recovery, you just wont be able to do these workouts more than once per 5 days. Once per 7 days is probably a LITTLE more rest than some people need, but I think its better to err on that side than the side of overtraining.
               </p>
               <p>
                The bottom line is EVERYONE that follows the program makes rapid strength and size gains. Even long-time lifters.
               </p>
               <p>
                That said, the one thing that we sometimes do is add 6 additional chest sets 3-4 days after the chest workout. You would do that first and then move on to your normal workout.
               </p>
              </div>
             </div>

                 <div class="dsq-comment-body" id="dsq-comment-body-11212">
                  <div class="dsq-comment-message" id="dsq-comment-message-11212">
                   <p>
                    Slow chest growth is probably the issue Im most often emailed about, haha. This is definitely the most common genetic weak point, and really just takes time and hard work to get through. In my opinion, it takes 1-2 years to build what we would consider a solid chest, and 3-4 to build an awesome chest.
                   </p>
                   <p>
                    Dont be discouraged by that though. As long as your diet is right and youre getting stronger each month, your chest WILL grow. It just grows slower than most people prefer.
                   </p>
                   <p>
                    Heres one little trick you can do:
                   </p>
                   <p>
                    On the 3rd day after chest day, start your workout with a chest warm-up and 3 heavy sets of incline barbell press. Then move on to your normal workout. This wont be enough to interfere with your next chest day, and can give you a little boost.
                   </p>
                  </div>
                 </div>

             </p>

              its flat dumbbell bench superset with dumbbell chest flies, then we go to incline dumbbell bench, then hammerstrength chest press superset with dips( as many as I can. For 8, Im doing like 70lb dumbbells for flat bench and for 6s Im doing 75 lb dumbbells but I have to drop down to 70 after the 2nd set.

               <ul class="children">
                <li class="comment odd alt depth-4" id="dsq-comment-10395">
                 <div class="dsq-comment-header" id="dsq-comment-header-10395">
                  <cite id="dsq-cite-10395">
                   <span id="dsq-author-user-10395">
                    Michael Matthews
                   </span>
                  </cite>
                 </div>
                 <div class="dsq-comment-body" id="dsq-comment-body-10395">
                  <div class="dsq-comment-message" id="dsq-comment-message-10395">
                   <p>
                    Regarding the spotting issue, what you want to do is end your bench, squat, and military press sets (the only exercises where you need a spot) with one rep still in the tankthat is, end your sets when you struggle for a rep and arent sure you can get another. You shouldnt need a spot for any other exercises.
                   </p>
                   <p>
                    Or you can always ask for someone else in the gym to give you a spot. I do it all the time. The last resort is the Smith Machine.
                    <img alt=":)" class="wp-smiley" src="http://muscleforlife-legionathletics.netdna-ssl.com/wp-includes/images/smilies/icon_smile.gif"/>
                   </p>
                   <p>
                    Otherwise you can do 3 more sets of incline DB presses.
                   </p>
                   <p>
                    You should see a difference in 2 months. Youre not going to build a massive chest in that time but it will be noticeable.
                   </p>
                   <p>
                    I like ~30 degrees. I find 45 too high.
                   </p>
                   <p>
                    Hope this helps! Talk soon!
                   </p>
                  </div>
                 </div>
                </li>

           <div class="dsq-comment-body" id="dsq-comment-body-10531">
            <div class="dsq-comment-message" id="dsq-comment-message-10531">
             <p>
              I used to be fearful of heavy reps on barbell exercise, and I can see in the comments that others have this fear as well.  The first time I failed to get a rep up without a spotter was actually a pretty liberating moment.  I found it extremely easy to simply roll the bar off me, set it on the floor, and then do a little deadlift/shrug motion to set the bar back on the pins.  Obviously better to leave one in the tank as you suggest, but I dont think there is any reason to be paralyzed by the fear of a barbell being stuck on your chest.
             </p>
            </div>

                    <div class="dsq-comment-message" id="dsq-comment-message-12426">
                     <p>
                      Thatd be interesting, never tried that. One thing I did try recently that I dont recommend is doing Shoulders/Tris. I tried putting Chest on its own day because it was lagging for me, but it ended up being too hard on my shoulders which are more prone to injury. Back/Bi &amp; Chest/Tri has worked great for me because there are some exercises that hit both like underhand/parallel grip chin-ups, barbell rows, close grip bench press. I cant think of anything that hits Chest &amp; Biceps quite as equally so youd end up doing more exercises and spending more time in the gym.
                     </p>

                  <li class="comment odd alt depth-5" id="dsq-comment-16966">
                   <div class="dsq-comment-header" id="dsq-comment-header-16966">
                    <cite id="dsq-cite-16966">
                     <span id="dsq-author-user-16966">
                      Michael
                     </span>
                    </cite>
                   </div>
                   <div class="dsq-comment-body" id="dsq-comment-body-16966">
                    <div class="dsq-comment-message" id="dsq-comment-message-16966">
                     <p>
                      Absolutley, Triceps and Shoulders together (even for the seasoned) just dont go well fo
                      <br/>
                      r me either. Alot of books/web pages do put these two (shoulder &amp; triceps) together. Esspecially beginer routines. For me, if I do my shoulders first, my tricep exercises lack reps and weight. If I do my triceps first, my shoulder work out stinks. Back &amp; Bis/Chest &amp; Tris, Shoulders &amp; Legs, work better for me.
                      <br/>
                      If your just starting Heavy Weight training, or are still weak, dont worry about your arms. The basic Back/Chest/Shoulder and Leg workouts posted here, are enough. Get form and Strenghth. Through acheivment of the basics, your arms will come in, then work Bis &amp; Tris in. Remember keep it simple
                      <br/>
                      Chow
                     </p>
                    </div>
                   </div>
                  </li>

          <li class="comment odd alt thread-odd thread-alt depth-1" id="dsq-comment-18355">
           <div class="dsq-comment-header" id="dsq-comment-header-18355">
            <cite id="dsq-cite-18355">
             <span id="dsq-author-user-18355">
              Nathan Rowe
             </span>
            </cite>
           </div>
           <div class="dsq-comment-body" id="dsq-comment-body-18355">
            <div class="dsq-comment-message" id="dsq-comment-message-18355">
             <p>
              From the published literature Ive seen it seems to say that incline bench does not affect the activation of clavicular pectoralis, see here:
             </p>
             <p>
              <a href="http://journals.lww.com/nsca-jscr/abstract/1995/11000/effects_of_variations_of_the_bench_press_exercise.3.aspx" rel="nofollow">
               http://journals.lww.com/nsca-jscr/abstract/1995/11000/effects_of_variations_of_the_bench_press_exercise.3.aspx
              </a>
             </p>
             <p>
              <a href="http://www.researchgate.net/publication/232218111_Electromyographical_Activity_of_the_Pectoralis_Muscle_During_Incline_and_Decline_Bench_Presses" rel="nofollow">
               http://www.researchgate.net/publication/232218111_Electromyographical_Activity_of_the_Pectoralis_Muscle_During_Incline_and_Decline_Bench_Presses
              </a>
             </p>
             <p>
              However, the first paper does state that moving to a narrower grip activates the clavicular head more.
             </p>
             <p>
              This paper says that the clavicular head is activated more with a supinated grip (hands turned so the palms face upwards):
             </p>
             <p>
              <a href="http://journals.lww.com/nsca-jscr/Abstract/2005/08000/The_Influence_of_Grip_Width_and_Forearm.17.aspx" rel="nofollow">
               http://journals.lww.com/nsca-jscr/Abstract/2005/08000/The_Influence_of_Grip_Width_and_Forearm.17.aspx
              </a>
             </p>
             <p>
              The first paper also states that the sternocostal (middle) portion of the chest is activated more on horizontal bench than decline.
             </p>
            </div>
           </div>
           <ul class="children">
            <li class="comment even depth-2" id="dsq-comment-18445">
             <div class="dsq-comment-header" id="dsq-comment-header-18445">
              <cite id="dsq-cite-18445">
               <span id="dsq-author-user-18445">
                Michael Matthews
               </span>
              </cite>
             </div>
             <div class="dsq-comment-body" id="dsq-comment-body-18445">
              <div class="dsq-comment-message" id="dsq-comment-message-18445">
               <p>
                Thanks for the comment! You should read the papers youre citing not just the abstracts.
               </p>
               <p>
                This paper shows the incline press activate the clavicular pectoralis more than the decline:
               </p>
               <p>
                <a href="http://journals.lww.com/nsca-jscr/abstract/1995/11000/effects_of_variations_of_the_bench_press_exercise.3.aspx" rel="nofollow">
                 http://journals.lww.com/nsca-jscr/abstract/1995/11000/effects_of_variations_of_the_bench_press_exercise.3.aspx
                </a>
               </p>
               <p>
                Trust meif you want to have a full, proportionate chest, you need to emphasize your incline pressing.
               </p>
              </div>
             </div>
             <ul class="children">
              <li class="comment odd alt depth-3" id="dsq-comment-18468">
               <div class="dsq-comment-header" id="dsq-comment-header-18468">
                <cite id="dsq-cite-18468">
                 <span id="dsq-author-user-18468">
                  Nathan Rowe
                 </span>
                </cite>
               </div>
               <div class="dsq-comment-body" id="dsq-comment-body-18468">
                <div class="dsq-comment-message" id="dsq-comment-message-18468">
                 <p>
                  That paper does show that the incline activates the clavicular pectoralis more than decline, but it says that there is no difference between incline and flat. With that in mind, would it not be better to focus on flat bench where you can lift more than on incline? Or focus on using a supinated grip to better engage the clavicular pectoralis?
                 </p>
                 <p>
                  There is a lot of anecdotal testimony that incline work builds the upper chest more than flat, and that does often turn into real published science (observations are made, hypotheses are formulated and tested), but from the literature I have read it does not seem to be because it activates the upper chest more. Maybe, there is another reason for it, is there anything else you can think of to explain it?
                 </p>
                </div>
               </div>

           </div>
           <ul class="children">
            <li class="comment even depth-2" id="dsq-comment-20306">
             <div class="dsq-comment-header" id="dsq-comment-header-20306">
              <cite id="dsq-cite-20306">
               <span id="dsq-author-user-20306">
                Michael Matthews
               </span>
              </cite>
             </div>
             <div class="dsq-comment-body" id="dsq-comment-body-20306">
              <div class="dsq-comment-message" id="dsq-comment-message-20306">
               <p>
                Hey man!
               </p>
               <p>
                I like increasing my weight once I hit 6 reps. So set 1, 6, add weight, get 4 or so next 2 sets, work with that weight next week until 6, go up, etc. If, however, you only get 2 to 3 reps after increasing, drop back and work with that lower weight until you can do TWO sets of 6, and then try to move up again. If that still fails, then work up to 3 sets of 6 and youll be fine.
               </p>
              </div>
             </div>
             <ul class="children">

             <p>
              Hi Michael, thanks for sharing your workout program, i have a few questions for you:)
             </p>
             <p>
              First I really dont like working chest with triceps so I do chest with back, and shoulders with full arms another day. I was said that doing two big muscles together isnt very good, I personally love it due to the high intensity it requires, I feel like I boost up my body every time, what do you think of it?
             </p>
             <p>
              Second, about my chest &amp; back workout, what I do is that I combine two exercises (chest&amp;back), this means that after my first series of chest, I do one series of back, this 3 times and then change exercises for both my chest and back.
              <br/>
              I also like it because it makes me sweat and I feel like I am doing sport! Not like doing one series and chill for 3min if you know what I mean. I know its quite different and people are quite surprise when they look at me working out since I never stop and I am the only one sweating.. do you think its bad for chest growth?
             </p>
             <p>
              Thanks for taking the time to read and keep up the good work!
             </p>

            <div class="dsq-comment-message" id="dsq-comment-message-23893">
             <p>
              Nice articles. I like your simple, basics approach. Its served me, and all I have trained, for over 3 1/2 decades. One trick I used to bring my upper chest up is Rack Incline Presses. Copied from my mentor, Mike Mentzer. Its an incredible upper-pec builder. 10-12 reps (due to the short 1/4 ROM), 5-secs. between reps rest the bar on pins. It allows numerous barely reps, and consistent progress.
             </p>
            </div>

            <div class="dsq-comment-message" id="dsq-comment-message-44640">
             <p>
              Why do you recommend keeping arms at 20 degrees relative to the torso on bench press? I understand reducing stress on the shoulders, but thats basically a close grip bench press and wouldnt engage the chest very much. Most people recommend 45-75 degrees and Ive always preferred a wider grip.
             </p>
            </div>
           </div>

              <div class="dsq-comment-message" id="dsq-comment-message-44730">
               <p>
                20 to 45 is the range. Some people tuck really close but Im closer to 35 to 40 degrees.
               </p>
               <p>
                45 to 75 degrees is terrible advice. 60+ with heavy weight is a shoulder injury waiting to happen.
               </p>

              </cite>
             </div>
             <div class="dsq-comment-body" id="dsq-comment-body-45495">
              <div class="dsq-comment-message" id="dsq-comment-message-45495">
               <p>
                I mean that the weight should be heavy enough where you cant get more than 6 but light enough where you dont do less than 4.
               </p>
               <p>
                I like increasing my weight once I hit 6 reps at a weight. So set 1, 6 reps, add weight, get 4 reps or so next 2 sets, work with that weight next week until I get it for 6 reps, go up, etc. If, however, you only get 2 to 3 reps after increasing, drop back and work with that lower weight until you can do TWO sets of 6, and then try to move up again. If that still fails, then work up to 3 sets of 6 and youll be fine.
               </p>
               <p>
                What do you think?
               </p>
              </div>
             </div>

             <p>
              Hi, Michael! Im very thin and my arms are really long. When I do the flat and inclined barbell bench press and let the barbell touch my chest, my anterior deltoids hurt. When I just put the barbell down until my arms form a 90-degree angle, it doesnt hurt that much. Also, when I do the closed-grip bench press and do the inclined dumbbell press the way you do, it doesnt hurt at all. Any insights?
             </p>
